Wellcamp quarantine facility 'a game of politics'

Publication info: Stock and Land ; Tullamarine, Vic. [Tullamarine, Vic]. 29 Aug 2021: 0.

https://login.ezproxy.lib.rmit.edu.au/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/wellcamp-quarantine-facility-game-politics/docview/2565527357/se-2?accountid=13552

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://libkey.io/libraries/1057/openurl?genre=article&amp;au=&amp;aulast=&amp;issn=13210157&amp;isbn=&amp;title=Wellcamp+quarantine+facility+%27a+game+of+politics%27&amp;jtitle=Stock+and+Land&amp;pubname=Stock+and+Land&amp;btitle=&amp;atitle=Wellcamp+quarantine+facility+%27a+game+of+politics%27&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=0&amp;date=2021&amp;doi=&amp;sid=ProQuest

Full text: The agreement between the Queensland government and Wagner Corporation to build a regional quarantine facility adjacent to Wellcamp Airport was dubbed 'a very important milestone' by Deputy Premier Steven Miles, but the community, local and federal governments aren't happy.
The announcement on Thursday morning came as a surprise to Toowoomba Regional Council Mayor Paul Antonio, who was not invited to the press conference at Wellcamp.
"I'm surprised that we haven't been on this journey, I'm surprised that we hadn't been told earlier," Cr Antonio said in his own press conference on Thursday afternoon.
"But in saying that, a decision has been made by the state government, they are legislatively able to make that kind of decision and I guess now the first thing I would ask is the state government visits Toowoomba as soon as they possibly can to give myself and my colleagues and this community an update."
Read more: Wellcamp quarantine facility to open this year Cr Antonio found out about the press conference by way of a phone call from one of the economic development groups in Toowoomba, asking if he had been invited to what was unfolding at Wellcamp.
"I happen to know a lot of things about the future of Toowoomba, some very exciting prospects that are coming our way, industry that's coming our way, and I naturally assumed that's what [the announcement] was," he said.
"I did ring the local federal member and he gave me the heads up, and then of course when I got back to my office my staff had been watching the TV, watching the announcement.
"It was very, very clear that we had been bypassed in the conversation and now we are having a quarantine facility being built here."
In response to the announcement, Cr Antonio said he clearly understands the concerns of many people in the Toowoomba area.
"You've only got to switch the TV on - today there's in excess of 1000 COVID infections in New South Wales, so COVID has the capacity to spread very quickly and I think anyone in Toowoomba who hears this news, naturally, would have some concerns about that," he said.
"The other thing I suggest to you is it will bring some economic benefit to Toowoomba and there will be some people who are certainly looking forward to that, but in saying all that, I think it is disappointing to me that we weren't brought on this journey.
"I would have been really pleased to share that, go on the journey and understand exactly what the state government had in mind.
"As a long-term, long-serving local government person, there's been a real lack of respect to this community and to myself personally, for me not knowing."
The mayor did however concede that a purpose-built facility was better than hotel quarantine.
"I think it's far better to have a hub similar to Howard Springs, and I think there's been no outbreaks from Howard Springs, and that sounds good to me," he said.
"That's better than having a CBD hub in Brisbane in a hotel facility that's not set up for it."
Ultimately, Cr Antonio says the Toowoomba community needs to be heard.
"I hope the state government is prepared to listen to some of the concerns of Toowoomba," he said.
"I would suggest also to [the community] that it's too late, this will happen.
"And it may be a good thing for the economy in Toowoomba. Let's hope there's no outbreaks, let's hope there's no problems with it, and let's hope that it's done well."
The Howard Springs model Mr Miles said the design and operation of the facility would be very closely modelled on the 'very successful' Howard Springs facility in the Northern Territory.
"It will be purpose built based on the learnings from that facility, therefore the infection control can keep our staff, the other people in the facility as well as the local community safe," he said.
"In fact, much much safer than non-purpose-built hotel quarantine accommodation where we currently have people.
"There will be a mix of single, double and family accommodation in cabin style with balconies, and importantly no hallways adjoining rooms which we have seen as vectors in hotel quarantine for the virus."
Mr Miles said they would continue to work through the details of the health and policing model that would be implemented at Wellcamp.
"COVID positive patients that require hospital care will be treated in one of the five COVID hospitals that we currently use, that we currently transfer people to," he said.
"By funding this facility ourselves and building this facility ourselves, we can ensure that it replaces current hotel quarantine usage that will allow us to take travellers that are within the cap and put them into this facility, reducing the need for hotel quarantine."
A game of politics In January, Queensland Country Life exclusively revealed Wagner Corporation and the Queensland government were in talks to build a quarantine hub.
The state government put the proposal to the federal government in a bid to have the project funded at the federal level, and from there 'a game of politics' ensued.
Prime Minister Scott Morrison eventually put the kibosh on the idea over allegations that the proposal for the facility lacked important 'detail' surrounding medical services and cost, and instead committed funding to a facility at Pinkenba.
Cr Antonio believes "there's a fair game of politics being played here", while Mr Miles has fired a shot at the federal government by saying "the work has started now, it's time for the politics to end".
Health Minister Yvette D'Ath also put the spotlight on the federal government.
"If this had been built months ago when we asked the Commonwealth to partner with us and the Wagners, we potentially may not have had to make the very difficult decision that we made yesterday to pause people domestically coming into hotel quarantine for a couple weeks," she said.
"But now we can move forward and we have this great facility that already construction is underway."
Ms D'Ath pointed to modelling from the Doherty Institute which 'has been talked about a lot' recently.
"Their own report says we will need to keep some public health measures in place - test, trace, isolate, quarantine - so even with high vaccination rates, we must continue these public health measures which means we will continue to need quarantine facilities and we need to make sure they're purpose built and they're keeping our community safe."
The lack of love between the state and federal leaders was clear throughout the press conference, but came to a precipice when one reporter asked Premier Annastacia Palaszczuk if the Prime Minister knew about the announcement.
"I'm quite sure he does now," the Premier responded.
Shortly after, the Prime Minister was asked about the announcement in his own press conference.
His response to the news that the Premier would fund the facility because his government wouldn't: "Well, she's been at liberty to do that for months."
"We've made it very clear that that facility did not seek to meet the national guidelines. And that's why we're going... forward together at Pinkenba," Mr Morrison said.
"But the Queensland government was always in a position to go ahead with that facility, if that's what they wish to do and to have people quarantine there rather than in hotel facilities.
"That is entirely a matter for the Queensland government, and they've made that decision.
"They could have done that months ago if that's what they wished to do. But good for them, and I wish them every success."
CREDIT: Hayley Kennedy

Stick to plan to reopen, Frydenberg tells states

Publication info: Sunday Age ; Melbourne, Vic. [Melbourne, Vic]. 29 Aug 2021: 1.

https://login.ezproxy.lib.rmit.edu.au/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/stick-plan-reopen-frydenberg-tells-states/docview/2565453640/se-2?accountid=13552

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://libkey.io/libraries/1057/openurl?genre=article&amp;au=&amp;aulast=&amp;issn=10341021&amp;isbn=&amp;title=Stick+to+plan+to+reopen%2C+Frydenberg+tells+states&amp;jtitle=Sunday+Age&amp;pubname=Sunday+Age&amp;btitle=&amp;atitle=Stick+to+plan+to+reopen%2C+Frydenberg+tells+states%3A+EXCLUSIVE&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=1&amp;date=2021&amp;doi=&amp;sid=ProQuest

Full text: Treasurer Josh Frydenberg has ratcheted up the pressure on state premiers to stick to the national plan to reopen once vaccination rates reach 70 and 80 per cent, warning the economy will suffer if they stray from the agreement.
In a coded warning to the resources states of Queensland and Western Australia - both of which have doggedly pursued COVID-zero policies and lag the rest of the country in vaccination rates - Mr Frydenberg also warned of a "ridiculous" scenario where it might be possible for many Australians to fly to Singapore or Canada, but not Perth or Cairns.
Amid ongoing lockdowns in Victoria, NSW and the ACT, Mr Frydenberg said economic growth would be "almost certainly negative in the September quarter, with a contraction of at least 2 per cent [but] I am hopeful and confident the economy can come back in the December quarter if, as expected, restrictions ease".
Economists are divided as to whether Wednesday's national accounts figures will show a quarter of negative growth in the three months to June 30. Two consecutive quarters of negative growth will signify the country is once again in a technical recession.
Mr Frydenberg warned that if premiers and chief ministers did not stick to the plan to reopen, then the hit to the economy and jobs would be high, with businesses closing and debt increasing.
But in the clearest signal yet that WA was prepared to walk away from the national reopening plan, Premier Mark McGowan said "we will need higher vaccination levels" and flagged waiting "a few more months" to reach them. However, Mr Frydenberg said: "Whatever the final number is on Wednesday, it does not change the fact that our economy faces some difficult days ahead. This is another reason why we must stick to our plan and open up at 70 and 80 per cent. If not now, when?"
AMP chief Economist Shane Oliver has forecast negative growth of 0.1 per cent for the June quarter, while Commonwealth Bank head of Australian economics Gareth Aird has forecast modest growth of 0.4 per cent. If the June quarter is negative, then Australia will almost certainly be in its second recession in as many years.
"Learning to live with the virus is our only hope. To delay and deny that fact is not only wrong but incredibly unrealistic," Mr Frydenberg said. "It's in no one's interests for states to be on different tracks when it comes to opening up. It would be ridiculous in the extreme to be able to travel to Canada but not Cairns, or Singapore but not Perth.
"The world is opening up, Australia will open up. Let's just make sure it's all states together, not just some. Opening up as one country and in accordance with the plan will not only give people hope but provide a springboard for our economic recovery."
Trade Minister Dan Tehan also stressed all states must stick to the plan agreed by national cabinet as he confirmed a travel bubble with Singapore would commence as soon as 80 per cent of Australians were fully vaccinated.
At a meeting on Friday, trade ministers agreed to finalise rules for the bubble by the end of the year so that when Australia hit its target, "Singapore will be ready to go", Mr Tehan said.
Last week, The Sunday Age revealed that according to updated Doherty Institute modelling, NSW would still be able to begin opening up once the vaccination targets were met - even if there were hundreds of new daily cases in the community, rather than the initially modelled 30.
That prompted some states, including WA and Queensland, to express concern about opening up and allowing COVID into the community.
At press conference yesterday, Mr McGowan gave the clearest signal yet he could delay opening up to the rest of the country until higher vaccination rates were reached. "We want to just remain COVID-free for as long as we can. Our vaccination levels at 70 per cent for instance, still means that 30 per cent of the adult population are unvaccinated. And then when you add kids on, that's about another 20 per cent," he said.
"So were we to deliberately import the virus at that point in time, hundreds of people would die, it would result in huge economic dislocation and for what? If we wait a few more months, we get our vaccination levels up to a higher level, we can avoid all of that."
Each week of lockdown in NSW and Victoria costs the economy an estimated $2 billion in lost output, while fiscal support from the federal government is worth hundreds of millions more per week. Mr Frydenberg has previously signalled that, for states that refuse to open up in line with the national plan, that financial support could be withdrawn.
Mr Oliver, the AMP economist who forecast negative growth in June and September, said that "in a purely economic sense, a recession doesn't mean much" but it could impact confidence, which in turn impacted the speed of
the recovery.
Mr Aird, the Commonwealth Bank economist, said that whether Australia officially entered recession later this year or not, "a huge number of people are stood down, the economy is going through a huge shock".
"Try telling someone in Victoria or NSW we aren't in recession ... however, we are almost certain to rebound in quarter four as things open up," he said.
Mr Frydenberg, who was last year a vocal critic of Victorian Premier Daniel Andrews' extended second wave lockdown, defended himself from criticism that he had not spoken out against NSW Premier Gladys Berejiklian's lockdown in the same way.
"My concern has always been first and foremost for the Victorian people, that is why I spoke up vigorously in their defence last year and that is why from day one of this pandemic I have ensured Victoria has received an unprecedented amount of economic support," said the MP for Kooyong in Melbourne's east.
"I have a good working relationship with my Victorian counterpart, Tim Pallas, and in the latest Victorians lockdown alone we have joined together to provide more than $2 billion in economic support."
Relations between the federal Liberal government and the state Labor government have eased somewhat. Mr Andrews recently noted the "federal Treasurer has taken a different tone and a different approach this year and I think Victorians are grateful for that".
With Michael Koziol
CREDIT: James Massola Sunday political correspondent

Frydenberg warns states to stick to the plan to reopen

Publication info: Sun Herald ; Sydney, N.S.W. [Sydney, N.S.W]. 29 Aug 2021: 5.

https://login.ezproxy.lib.rmit.edu.au/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/frydenberg-warns-states-stick-plan-reopen/docview/2565452803/se-2?accountid=13552

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://libkey.io/libraries/1057/openurl?genre=article&amp;au=&amp;aulast=&amp;issn=13231987&amp;isbn=&amp;title=Frydenberg+warns+states+to+stick+to+the+plan+to+reopen&amp;jtitle=Sun+Herald&amp;pubname=Sun+Herald&amp;btitle=&amp;atitle=Frydenberg+warns+states+to+stick+to+the+plan+to+reopen%3A+exclusive&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=5&amp;date=2021&amp;doi=&amp;sid=ProQuest

Full text: Federal Treasurer Josh Frydenberg has ratcheted up the pressure on state premiers to stick to the national plan to reopen once vaccination rates reach 70 and 80 per cent, warning the country's economy will suffer if they stray from the agreement.
In a coded warning to the resources states of Queensland and Western Australia - both of which have doggedly pursued COVID-zero policies and which are at the back of the pack for vaccination rates - Mr Frydenberg also warned of a "ridiculous" scenario in which it might be possible for many Australians to fly to Singapore or Canada, but not Perth or Cairns.
Economists are divided on whether Wednesday's national accounts figures will show a quarter of negative growth in the three months to June 30.
Mr Frydenberg said: "Whatever the final number is on Wednesday, it does not change the fact that our economy faces some difficult days ahead. This is another reason why we must stick to our plan and open up at 70 and 80 per cent. If not now, when?"
He warned that, if premiers and chief ministers did not stick to the plan to reopen, then the hit to the economy and jobs would be high, with businesses closing and debt increasing.
But in the clearest signal yet that Western Australia was prepared to walk away from the national reopening plan, Premier Mark McGowan said "we will need higher vaccination levels" and flagged waiting "a few more months" to reach them.
Amid ongoing lockdowns in NSW, Victoria and the ACT in the current, third quarter of the financial year, Mr Frydenberg said growth would be "almost certainly negative in the September quarter, with a contraction of at least 2 per cent, [but] I am hopeful and confident the economy can come back in the December quarter if, as expected, restrictions ease".
AMP chief economist Shane Oliver has forecast negative growth of 0.1 per cent for the June quarter, while Commonwealth Bank head of Australian economics Gareth Aird has forecast modest growth of 0.4 per cent. If the June quarter is negative, then Australia will almost certainly be in its second recession in as many years.
"Learning to live with the virus is our only hope. To delay and deny that fact is not only wrong but incredibly unrealistic," Mr Frydenberg said.
"It's in no one's interests for states to be on different tracks when it comes to opening up. It would be ridiculous in the extreme to be able to travel to Canada but not Cairns, or Singapore but not Perth.
"The world is opening up, Australia will open up. Let's just make sure it's all states together, not just some. Opening up as one country and in accordance with the plan will not only give people hope but provide a springboard for our economic recovery."
Last week, The Sun-Herald revealed that, according to updated Doherty Institute modelling, NSW would still be able to begin opening up once the vaccination targets were met - even if there were hundreds of new daily cases in the community, rather than the initially modelled 30.
That prompted some states, including Western Australia and Queensland, to express concern about opening up and allowing COVID into the community.
At press conference yesterday, Mr McGowan gave the clearest signal yet he could delay opening up to the rest of the country until higher vaccination rates are reached.
"We want to just remain COVID-free for as long as we can. Our vaccination levels at 70 per cent, for instance, still means that 30 per cent of the adult population are unvaccinated. And then, when you add kids on, that's about another 20 per cent," he said.
"So were we to deliberately import the virus at that point in time, hundreds of people would die, it would result in huge economic dislocation and for what? If we wait a few more months, we get our vaccination levels up to a higher level, we can avoid all of that."
Each week of lockdown in NSW and Victoria costs the economy an estimated $2 billion in lost output, while fiscal support from the federal government is worth hundreds of millions more per week. Mr Frydenberg has previously signalled that, for states that refuse to open up in line with the national plan, that financial support could be withdrawn.
Mr Oliver, the AMP economist who forecast negative growth in June and September said that "in a purely economic sense, a recession doesn't mean much" but it could impact confidence, which in turn impacts the speed of the recovery. He said the "recession" concept was less meaningful than usual as it was not a normal cyclical recession and the economy should recover more quickly than would occur after a normal recession.
Mr Aird, the Commonwealth Bank economist, said that whether Australia officially entered recession later this year or not "a huge number of people are stood down, the economy is going through a huge shock".
"Try telling someone in Victoria or NSW we aren't in recession ... however, we are almost certain to rebound in quarter four as things open up."
CREDIT: James Massola

THE LIMBO MUST END FOR SAKE OF NATION

Publication info: The Gold Coast Bulletin ; Southport, Qld. [Southport, Qld]. 28 Aug 2021: 64.

https://login.ezproxy.lib.rmit.edu.au/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/limbo-must-end-sake-nation/docview/2565260054/se-2?accountid=13552

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://libkey.io/libraries/1057/openurl?genre=article&amp;au=&amp;aulast=&amp;issn=13213830&amp;isbn=&amp;title=THE+LIMBO+MUST+END+FOR+SAKE+OF+NATION&amp;jtitle=The+Gold+Coast+Bulletin&amp;pubname=The+Gold+Coast+Bulletin&amp;btitle=&amp;atitle=THE+LIMBO+MUST+END+FOR+SAKE+OF+NATION&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=64&amp;date=2021&amp;doi=&amp;sid=ProQuest

Full text: THE Premier has locked out rate-paying citizens. Many are stuck in limbo for work or on a break around the country, not allowed to return home.
The state government is using just 22 hotels for quarantine. It’s ludicrous considering the occupancy rate in Gold Coast hotels is at a desperate low. This move spreads separation and fear and pits Queenslanders against the rest of the country.
What we need is safety and hope. It’s time Australians joined many other countries and learned to live with Covid.
Lockdowns are not sustainable. The emotional distress and economic damage to our communities, especially at the border, is too great a burden. We want and need our lives and freedoms back.
Delta zero is unrealistic. Elimination has never been the strategy. Even through strict lockdowns in NSW, community transmission continues. Importantly, the report on Friday from NSW outlined that 103 of 117 people in ICU are unvaccinated.
Underpinned by Doherty Institute modelling, the road to safety and hope has been created through the National Plan. The Premier has agreed to it in principle, at national cabinet.
The first phase is well underway with vaccinations accelerating. One million doses have been administered in just three days, outperforming the US and the UK, per capita.
The second phase is where hope grows. At 70 per cent of 16+ double jabbed, perhaps November, certainly December, easing of restrictions will allow domestic travel to resume for our tourism industry, local businesses and those families who rely on it. Part of that transition phase will be travel incentives for those who are vaccinated or the choice to quarantine for those who are not.
Phase three sees vaccination rates at 80 per cent. Here, we’ll be able to live with the virus in our community. The new reality where international travel can resume will allow a restart for the international education sector on the Gold Coast. Arrival caps and testing will be in place.
In phase four, Covid-19 will be managed as an infectious disease like any other. Annual vaccination boosters will be necessary, as they are now with flu.
Australians have proven throughout history that we are brave and resilient enough to endure whatever crises we face as a nation. Queenslanders and Gold Coasters are no exception.
The most pressing question must be asked: Will the Premier keep her word? Can she find the stuff to lay down the political weapon of border closures that has served her popularity so well?
If she resists, the public will rightly ask why travel and Tweed residents are still being denied when we have done what we’ve been asked.
If we don’t follow the plan to end restrictions at 70 and 80 per cent to reunite Australians, and reclaim our way of life, when can we Premier? Federal Member for Moncrieff
CREDIT: ANGIE BELL

____________________________________________________________

Wellcamp quarantine facility 'a game of politics'

Publication info: Queensland Country Life ; Brisbane, Qld. [Brisbane, Qld]. 27 Aug 2021: 0.

https://login.ezproxy.lib.rmit.edu.au/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/wellcamp-quarantine-facility-game-politics/docview/2564556258/se-2?accountid=13552

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://libkey.io/libraries/1057/openurl?genre=article&amp;au=&amp;aulast=&amp;issn=1321165X&amp;isbn=&amp;title=Wellcamp+quarantine+facility+%27a+game+of+politics%27&amp;jtitle=Queensland+Country+Life&amp;pubname=Queensland+Country+Life&amp;btitle=&amp;atitle=Wellcamp+quarantine+facility+%27a+game+of+politics%27&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=0&amp;date=2021&amp;doi=&amp;sid=ProQuest

Full text: The agreement between the Queensland government and Wagner Corporation to build a regional quarantine facility adjacent to Wellcamp Airport was dubbed 'a very important milestone' by Deputy Premier Steven Miles, but the community, local and federal governments aren't happy.
The announcement on Thursday morning came as a surprise to Toowoomba Regional Council Mayor Paul Antonio, who was not invited to the press conference at Wellcamp.
"I'm surprised that we haven't been on this journey, I'm surprised that we hadn't been told earlier," Cr Antonio said in his own press conference on Thursday afternoon.
"But in saying that, a decision has been made by the state government, they are legislatively able to make that kind of decision and I guess now the first thing I would ask is the state government visits Toowoomba as soon as they possibly can to give myself and my colleagues and this community an update."
Read more: Wellcamp quarantine facility to open this year Cr Antonio found out about the press conference by way of a phone call from one of the economic development groups in Toowoomba, asking if he had been invited to what was unfolding at Wellcamp.
"I happen to know a lot of things about the future of Toowoomba, some very exciting prospects that are coming our way, industry that's coming our way, and I naturally assumed that's what [the announcement] was," he said.
"I did ring the local federal member and he gave me the heads up, and then of course when I got back to my office my staff had been watching the TV, watching the announcement.
"It was very, very clear that we had been bypassed in the conversation and now we are having a quarantine facility being built here."
In response to the announcement, Cr Antonio said he clearly understands the concerns of many people in the Toowoomba area.
"You've only got to switch the TV on - today there's in excess of 1000 COVID infections in New South Wales, so COVID has the capacity to spread very quickly and I think anyone in Toowoomba who hears this news, naturally, would have some concerns about that," he said.
"The other thing I suggest to you is it will bring some economic benefit to Toowoomba and there will be some people who are certainly looking forward to that, but in saying all that, I think it is disappointing to me that we weren't brought on this journey.
"I would have been really pleased to share that, go on the journey and understand exactly what the state government had in mind.
"As a long-term, long-serving local government person, there's been a real lack of respect to this community and to myself personally, for me not knowing."
The mayor did however concede that a purpose-built facility was better than hotel quarantine.
"I think it's far better to have a hub similar to Howard Springs, and I think there's been no outbreaks from Howard Springs, and that sounds good to me," he said.
"That's better than having a CBD hub in Brisbane in a hotel facility that's not set up for it."
Ultimately, Cr Antonio says the Toowoomba community needs to be heard.
"I hope the state government is prepared to listen to some of the concerns of Toowoomba," he said.
"I would suggest also to [the community] that it's too late, this will happen.
"And it may be a good thing for the economy in Toowoomba. Let's hope there's no outbreaks, let's hope there's no problems with it, and let's hope that it's done well."
The Howard Springs model Mr Miles said the design and operation of the facility would be very closely modelled on the 'very successful' Howard Springs facility in the Northern Territory.
"It will be purpose built based on the learnings from that facility, therefore the infection control can keep our staff, the other people in the facility as well as the local community safe," he said.
"In fact, much much safer than non-purpose-built hotel quarantine accommodation where we currently have people.
"There will be a mix of single, double and family accommodation in cabin style with balconies, and importantly no hallways adjoining rooms which we have seen as vectors in hotel quarantine for the virus."
Mr Miles said they would continue to work through the details of the health and policing model that would be implemented at Wellcamp.
"COVID positive patients that require hospital care will be treated in one of the five COVID hospitals that we currently use, that we currently transfer people to," he said.
"By funding this facility ourselves and building this facility ourselves, we can ensure that it replaces current hotel quarantine usage that will allow us to take travellers that are within the cap and put them into this facility, reducing the need for hotel quarantine."
A game of politics In January, Queensland Country Life exclusively revealed Wagner Corporation and the Queensland government were in talks to build a quarantine hub.
The state government put the proposal to the federal government in a bid to have the project funded at the federal level, and from there 'a game of politics' ensued.
Prime Minister Scott Morrison eventually put the kibosh on the idea over allegations that the proposal for the facility lacked important 'detail' surrounding medical services and cost, and instead committed funding to a facility at Pinkenba.
Cr Antonio believes "there's a fair game of politics being played here", while Mr Miles has fired a shot at the federal government by saying "the work has started now, it's time for the politics to end".
Health Minister Yvette D'Ath also put the spotlight on the federal government.
"If this had been built months ago when we asked the Commonwealth to partner with us and the Wagners, we potentially may not have had to make the very difficult decision that we made yesterday to pause people domestically coming into hotel quarantine for a couple weeks," she said.
"But now we can move forward and we have this great facility that already construction is underway."
Ms D'Ath pointed to modelling from the Doherty Institute which 'has been talked about a lot' recently.
"Their own report says we will need to keep some public health measures in place - test, trace, isolate, quarantine - so even with high vaccination rates, we must continue these public health measures which means we will continue to need quarantine facilities and we need to make sure they're purpose built and they're keeping our community safe."
The lack of love between the state and federal leaders was clear throughout the press conference, but came to a precipice when one reporter asked Premier Annastacia Palaszczuk if the Prime Minister knew about the announcement.
"I'm quite sure he does now," the Premier responded.
Shortly after, the Prime Minister was asked about the announcement in his own press conference.
His response to the news that the Premier would fund the facility because his government wouldn't: "Well, she's been at liberty to do that for months."
"We've made it very clear that that facility did not seek to meet the national guidelines. And that's why we're going... forward together at Pinkenba," Mr Morrison said.
"But the Queensland government was always in a position to go ahead with that facility, if that's what they wish to do and to have people quarantine there rather than in hotel facilities.
"That is entirely a matter for the Queensland government, and they've made that decision.
"They could have done that months ago if that's what they wished to do. But good for them, and I wish them every success."

Younger teens to join the vaccination race

Publication info: Newcastle Herald ; Newcastle, N.S.W. [Newcastle, N.S.W]. 27 Aug 2021: 10.

https://login.ezproxy.lib.rmit.edu.au/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/younger-teens-join-vaccination-race/docview/2564555645/se-2?accountid=13552

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://libkey.io/libraries/1057/openurl?genre=article&amp;au=&amp;aulast=&amp;issn=22028021&amp;isbn=&amp;title=Younger+teens+to+join+the+vaccination+race&amp;jtitle=Newcastle+Herald&amp;pubname=Newcastle+Herald&amp;btitle=&amp;atitle=Younger+teens+to+join+the+vaccination+race&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=10&amp;date=2021&amp;doi=&amp;sid=ProQuest

Full text: A plan to vaccinate children aged 12 to 15 is set to be unveiled on Friday, as the national cabinet plots the next steps in the plan to end lockdowns and heavy coronavirus restrictions.
Prime Minister Scott Morrison on Thursday confirmed that 1.2 million Australian children would be added to the rollout, pending final advice from its expert vaccines panel ATAGI.
The head of the vaccine rollout, Lieutenant-General John Frewen, briefed cabinet's national security committee on the plan on Thursday.
It will be put to state and territory leaders for approval at Friday's national cabinet meeting.
Leaders will also discuss what exemptions vaccinated people might be granted once Australia hits the 70 per cent and 80 per cent vaccination targets agreed to under the national roadmap.
Mr Morrison has this week stepped up pressure on the states to hold true to the plan, amid growing doubts over whether the Doherty Institute modelling which underpins the roadmap can still be relied upon given the worsening Delta outbreaks in Sydney and Melbourne.
The Prime Minister said on Thursday morning that with some 1.8 million doses now being administered across Australia each week, the addition of children aged 12 to 15 was "well within the capability of the vaccination program".
A rollout record 335,420 doses were administered in the most recent 24-hour period, with more than 32 per of over-16s now fully vaccinated.
The ATAGI panel has already approved the Pfizer vaccine for at-risk 12- to 15-year-olds, but has yet to make a final call on its use for other adolescents.
While the cohort is set to become eligible for the jab, it will not be included in the 70 per cent and 80 per cent community-wide targets, which need to be hit before restrictions start to ease under the national reopening plan.
ACT Chief Minister Andrew Barr has been pushing for 12- to 15-year-olds to be added to the national rollout, as Delta outbreaks in Canberra, Sydney and Melbourne all strike proportionately high numbers of younger, mostly unvaccinated, people.
Mr Barr confirmed on Thursday that the ACT would deviate from the national strategy and include 12- to 15-year-olds in its own vaccination targets.
Labor's Mark Butler said with countries such as the US and Canada vaccinating 12- to 15-year-olds as far back as May, Mr Morrison needed to explain why his government had dragged its feet in protecting younger Australians.
CREDIT: Dan Jervis-Bardy

Young teens soon to join vaccine race

https://login.ezproxy.lib.rmit.edu.au/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/young-teens-soon-join-vaccine-race/docview/2564552272/se-2?accountid=13552

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://libkey.io/libraries/1057/openurl?genre=article&amp;au=&amp;aulast=&amp;issn=01576925&amp;isbn=&amp;title=Young+teens+soon+to+join+vaccine+race&amp;jtitle=The+Canberra+Times&amp;pubname=The+Canberra+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;atitle=Young+teens+soon+to+join+vaccine+race&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=&amp;date=2021&amp;doi=&amp;sid=ProQuest

Full text: A plan to vaccinate children aged 12 to 15 is set to be unveiled on Friday, as the national cabinet plots the next steps in the plan to end lockdowns and heavy coronavirus restrictions.
Prime Minister Scott Morrison on Thursday confirmed that 1.2 million Australian children would be added to the rollout, pending final advice from its expert vaccines panel ATAGI.
The head of the vaccine rollout, Lieutenant-General John Frewen, briefed cabinet's national security committee on the plan on Thursday.
It will be put to state and territory leaders for approval at Friday's national cabinet meeting.
Leaders will also discuss what exemptions vaccinated people might be granted once Australia hits the 70 per cent and 80 per cent vaccination targets agreed to under the national roadmap.
Mr Morrison has this week stepped up pressure on the states to hold true to the plan, amid growing doubts over whether the Doherty Institute modelling which underpins the roadmap can still be relied upon given the worsening Delta outbreaks in Sydney and Melbourne.
The Prime Minister said on Thursday morning that with some 1.8 million doses now being administered across Australia each week, the addition of children aged 12 to 15 was "well within the capability of the vaccination program".
A rollout record 335,420 doses were administered in the most recent 24-hour period, with more than 32 per of over-16s now fully vaccinated.
The ATAGI panel has already approved the Pfizer vaccine for at-risk 12- to 15-year-olds, but has yet to make a final call on its use for other adolescents.
While the cohort is set to become eligible for the jab, it will not be included in the 70 per cent and 80 per cent community-wide targets, which need to be hit before restrictions start to ease under the national reopening plan. ACT Chief Minister Andrew Barr has been pushing for 12- to 15-year-olds to be added to the national rollout, as Delta outbreaks in Canberra, Sydney and Melbourne all strike proportionately high numbers of younger, mostly unvaccinated, people. About 38 per cent of the cases in Canberra's outbreak are people aged under 17.
Mr Barr confirmed on Thursday that the ACT would deviate from the national strategy and include 12- to 15-year-olds in its own vaccination targets.
Labor's Mark Butler said with countries such as the US and Canada vaccinating 12- to 15-year-olds as far back as May, Mr Morrison needed to explain why his government had dragged its feet in protecting a cohort which had been on the the "front line" of Australia's "third wave".
"We deserve - parents deserve - a clear commitment from Scott Morrison about the rate of vaccination for Australia's high school students as we move to the next phases of the national plan," he said.
Greens health spokeswoman Rachel Siewert said it was "sickening" that children weren't included in the vaccination targets.
CREDIT: Dan Jervis-Bardy

Infection case spike hits record territory

Publication info: The Australian ; Canberra, A.C.T. [Canberra, A.C.T]. 25 Aug 2021: 5.

https://login.ezproxy.lib.rmit.edu.au/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/infection-case-spike-hits-record-territory/docview/2563919263/se-2?accountid=13552

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://libkey.io/libraries/1057/openurl?genre=article&amp;au=&amp;aulast=&amp;issn=10388761&amp;isbn=&amp;title=Infection+case+spike+hits+record+territory&amp;jtitle=The+Australian&amp;pubname=The+Australian&amp;btitle=&amp;atitle=Infection+case+spike+hits+record+territory&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=5&amp;date=2021&amp;doi=&amp;sid=ProQuest

Full text: The ACT has recorded its biggest spike in Covid-19 cases since the territory’s Delta outbreak began nearly two weeks ago, with total infections rising by 30 to 167.
Chief Minister Andrew Barr on Tuesday said four people were in hospital and one of them, a 40-year-old woman, was in intensive care.
Only 13 of the 30 new cases were in quarantine for their entire infectious period, while 11 were infectious in the community. Six cases remain under investigation, with more than 400 exposure sites across the ACT.
ACT chief health officer Kerryn Coleman said while the high number of cases might have come as a shock to many Canberrans, it was not necessarily surprising to health authorities.
She said it was a reminder of how fast the Delta strain of the virus could move through the community. “Specifically of these new cases today, I can confirm that out of the 30, 25 can be linked to known cases or ongoing clusters,” Dr Coleman said.
“We know that 21 of the 25 are actually household contacts, and the remaining four come from ­existing clusters.” She said the woman in intensive care had not been vaccinated and urged those living in the ACT to book an appointment to get immunised.
Mr Barr echoed the call. “The more Canberrans who are vaccinated, the better protected our community will be from the virus and from the decisions of other governments that are beyond the control of the ACT government,” he said.
“Vaccination remains absolutely critical in stopping the spread of the virus and protecting our community.” He reiterated his desire for the territory to achieve a higher vaccination rate than the 70 to 80 per cent figure flagged by Doherty Institute modelling as a threshold to start easing restrictions.
More than 60 per cent of Canberra adults have now had at least one jab. Mr Barr said it was too early to speculate on how long the lockdown – which is scheduled to end on September 2 – would last.
CREDIT: OLIVIA CAISLEY

Subject: Immunization; Intensive care

Title: Infection case spike hits record territory

Publication title: The Australian; Canberra, A.C.T.

First page: 5

Publication year: 2021

Publication date: Aug 25, 2021

Section: TheNation

Publisher: Nationwide News Pty Ltd

Place of publication: Canberra, A.C.T.

Country of publication: Australia, Canberra, A.C.T.

Publication subject: General Interest Periodicals--Australia

ISSN: 10388761

Source type: Newspaper

Language of publication: English

Infection case spike hits record territory

Publication info: The Australian ; Canberra, A.C.T. [Canberra, A.C.T]. 25 Aug 2021: 5.

https://login.ezproxy.lib.rmit.edu.au/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/infection-case-spike-hits-record-territory/docview/2563917387/se-2?accountid=13552

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://libkey.io/libraries/1057/openurl?genre=article&amp;au=&amp;aulast=&amp;issn=10388761&amp;isbn=&amp;title=Infection+case+spike+hits+record+territory&amp;jtitle=The+Australian&amp;pubname=The+Australian&amp;btitle=&amp;atitle=Infection+case+spike+hits+record+territory&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=5&amp;date=2021&amp;doi=&amp;sid=ProQuest

Full text: The ACT has recorded its biggest spike in Covid-19 cases since the territory’s Delta outbreak began nearly two weeks ago, with total infections rising by 30 to 167.
Chief Minister Andrew Barr on Tuesday said four people were in hospital and one of them, a 40-year-old woman, was in intensive care.
Only 13 of the 30 new cases were in quarantine for their entire infectious period, while 11 were infectious in the community. Six cases remain under investigation, with more than 400 exposure sites across the ACT.
ACT chief health officer Kerryn Coleman said while the high number of cases might have come as a shock to many Canberrans, it was not necessarily surprising to health authorities.
She said it was a reminder of how fast the Delta strain of the virus could move through the community.
“Specifically of these new cases today, I can confirm that out of the 30, 25 can be linked to known cases or ongoing clusters,” Dr Coleman said.
“We know that 21 of the 25 are actually household contacts, and the remaining four come from ­existing clusters.” She said the woman in intensive care had not been vaccinated and urged those living in the ACT to book an appointment to get immunised.
Mr Barr echoed the call.
“The more Canberrans who are vaccinated, the better protected our community will be from the virus and from the decisions of other governments that are beyond the control of the ACT government,” he said.
“Vaccination remains absolutely critical in stopping the spread of the virus and protecting our community.” He reiterated his desire for the territory to achieve a higher vaccination rate than the 70 to 80 per cent figure flagged by Doherty Institute modelling as a threshold to start easing restrictions.
CREDIT: OLIVIA CAISLEY

Targets not clear enough: expert

Publication info: The Courier ; Ballarat, Vic. [Ballarat, Vic]. 25 Aug 2021: 4.

https://login.ezproxy.lib.rmit.edu.au/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/targets-not-clear-enough-expert/docview/2563825457/se-2?accountid=13552

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://libkey.io/libraries/1057/openurl?genre=article&amp;au=&amp;aulast=&amp;issn=10370374&amp;isbn=&amp;title=Targets+not+clear+enough%3A+expert&amp;jtitle=The+Courier&amp;pubname=The+Courier&amp;btitle=&amp;atitle=Targets+not+clear+enough%3A+expert&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=4&amp;date=2021&amp;doi=&amp;sid=ProQuest

Full text: From page 1 In her personal opinion, children should have the vaccine.
Professor MacIntyre said all virus modelling systems, whether it be the ANU report or Grattan Institute or Doherty Institute, worked on assumptions and this was not always clear.
"If only 70 to 80 per cent of eligible people are vaccinated, that number translates to 60 to 64 per cent of the whole population. That matters," she said. "...That's not enough against Delta without some restrictions.
"We learnt that lesson from the UK, US and Israel which, although all countries had high vaccination rates, lifted restrictions in May this year and had massive COVID outbreaks. They've had to bring back non-pharmaceutical measures, like mask mandates. We won't be able to lift all restrictions."
Ballarat has 35.4 per cent of residents aged 15-plus fully vaccinated with access only starting to open up to under-40s. This is higher than the Australian jab rate of 30.9 per cent.
Committee for Ballarat chief Michael Poulton welcomed the opening-up of jabs to younger people but urged those who already had bookings to keep them, rather than join the queue.
Mr Poulton hoped young people would help drive the city's general jab rates higher by setting an example.
"It's terrific young people have been given the opportunity and it's terrific to have so much enthusiasm from younger generations in getting the vaccine," Mr Poulton said. "...If a 16-year-old has a conversation about getting a vaccination, it might make a collaborative opportunity to book in the whole family."

Political push heating up, but is it what voters want?

Publication info: The Age ; Melbourne, Vic. [Melbourne, Vic]. 23 Aug 2021: 4.

https://login.ezproxy.lib.rmit.edu.au/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/political-push-heating-up-is-what-voters-want/docview/2563298376/se-2?accountid=13552

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://libkey.io/libraries/1057/openurl?genre=article&amp;au=&amp;aulast=&amp;issn=2203580X&amp;isbn=&amp;title=Political+push+heating+up%2C+but+is+it+what+voters+want%3F&amp;jtitle=The+Age&amp;pubname=The+Age&amp;btitle=&amp;atitle=Political+push+heating+up%2C+but+is+it+what+voters+want%3F&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=4&amp;date=2021&amp;doi=&amp;sid=ProQuest

Full text: Politicians will have a temperature sensor put to their foreheads today when they turn up to federal Parliament to begin two weeks of debate with tempers flaring over the pandemic.
It is safe to expect some heat readings to be off the charts.
The Parliament is split, like the community, over the best way out of lockdowns that have sent employers into hibernation, put the economy into reverse and forced millions into their homes.
But there may be no better time for Parliament to meet.
The very fact of the sitting is a message in itself. Rather than try to suspend Parliament, the government kept to the timetable even though it meant putting MPs from all sides in isolation.
It is even more important when questions about the pandemic are being restricted to daily press conferences featuring premiers, chief health officers and journalists who are routinely scolded for challenging claims. The usual forums for debate have fallen silent: the Victorian Parliament is suspended and the NSW Parliament is not sitting.
That creates an opportunity for federal Labor leader Anthony Albanese to subject Prime Minister Scott Morrison to scrutiny over the risk of relaxing lockdown rules when vaccination rates hit 70 and 80 per cent. Morrison insists he is right on the national cabinet plan but is not releasing the latest advice from the Doherty Institute on its modelling. But the time for secrets is over.
With voters anxious ahead of an election, Morrison is wary of being direct. Asked a question, he can be a little like the Leyland brothers - travelling all over the countryside before he gets to the point.
The spear thrower for the government is Treasurer Josh Frydenberg, who has been speaking plainly in the past few days about what "living with the virus" means.
"What we have to do is be honest with the public that by living with COVID there'll be cases, by living with COVID there'll be people in hospital, and living with COVID there'll be deaths," Frydenberg says.
"But at the same time getting high levels of vaccination reduces the likelihood that lockdowns will be required and reduces the likelihood that people who get vaccinated will get sick."
Morrison kept that up yesterday by saying case numbers should not be the main measure of the pandemic and it was time to focus on serious illness and hospitalisation, which are lower thanks to vaccinations.
It is a difficult conversation at a time of real strain on the nation. With so much of the country shut, the Parliament may even serve a useful purpose. If the politicians can get past the temperature test.
CREDIT: David Crowe

Subject: Immunization; Parliaments; Pandemics

Heated debate might be just what we need

Publication info: Sydney Morning Herald ; Sydney, N.S.W. [Sydney, N.S.W]. 23 Aug 2021: 5.

https://login.ezproxy.lib.rmit.edu.au/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/heated-debate-might-be-just-what-we-need/docview/2563298121/se-2?accountid=13552

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://libkey.io/libraries/1057/openurl?genre=article&amp;au=&amp;aulast=&amp;issn=03126315&amp;isbn=&amp;title=Heated+debate+might+be+just+what+we+need&amp;jtitle=Sydney+Morning+Herald&amp;pubname=Sydney+Morning+Herald&amp;btitle=&amp;atitle=Heated+debate+might+be+just+what+we+need&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=5&amp;date=2021&amp;doi=&amp;sid=ProQuest

Full text: Politicians will have a temperature sensor put to their foreheads today when they turn up to federal Parliament to begin two weeks of debate with tempers flaring over the pandemic.
It is safe to expect some heat readings to be off the charts.
The Parliament is split, like the community, over the best way out of lockdowns that have sent employers into hibernation, put the economy into reverse and forced millions into their homes.
But there may be no better time for Parliament to meet. Australians have low expectations for their politicians but the sitting comes at a critical point in the argument about when to ease the lockdown rules.
The very fact of the sitting is a message in itself. Rather than try to suspend Parliament, the government kept to the timetable even though it meant putting MPs from all sides in isolation for two weeks before the sitting.
To do otherwise, and give up, would have fatally undermined the message that the country has to "live with the virus" and get on with things.
It is even more important when questions about the pandemic are being restricted to daily press conferences featuring premiers, chief health officers and journalists who are routinely scolded for stepping out of line. The usual forums for debate have fallen silent: the Victorian Parliament is suspended and the NSW Parliament is not sitting, although some committees continue.
That creates an opportunity for federal Labor leader Anthony Albanese to subject Prime Minister Scott Morrison to scrutiny over the risk of relaxing lockdown rules when vaccination rates hit 70 and 80 per cent.
Morrison insists he is right on the national cabinet plan but is not releasing the latest advice from the Doherty Institute on its modelling. But the time for keeping secrets is over. Every assessment needs to be made public.
Other voices in the government are making a strong case that it is time to level with voters. Some Liberals want to stiffen the Prime Minister's spine and make sure the premiers and chief ministers do not force a federal retreat. With voters anxious ahead of an election, Morrison is wary of being direct. Asked a question, he can be a little like the Leyland brothers - travelling all over the countryside before he gets to the point.
The spear thrower for the government is Treasurer Josh Frydenberg, who has been speaking plainly in the past few days about what "living with the virus" really means.
"What we have to do is be honest with the public that by living with COVID there'll be cases, by living with COVID there'll be people in hospital, and living with COVID there'll be deaths," Frydenberg says. "But at the same time getting high levels of vaccination reduces the likelihood that lockdowns will be required and reduces the likelihood that people who get vaccinated will get sick."
Morrison kept that up yesterday by saying case numbers should not be the main measure of the pandemic and it was time to focus on serious illness and hospitalisation, which are lower thanks to vaccinations.
It is a difficult conversation at a time of real strain on the nation, its leaders and its people. With so much of the country shut, the Parliament may even serve a useful purpose. If the politicians can get past the temperature test.
CREDIT: David Crowe

Subject: Immunization; Parliaments; Politics; Pandemics

Herald Sun

Publication info: Herald Sun ; Melbourne, Vic. [Melbourne, Vic]. 21 Aug 2021: 50.

https://login.ezproxy.lib.rmit.edu.au/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/herald-sun/docview/2562990101/se-2?accountid=13552

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://libkey.io/libraries/1057/openurl?genre=article&amp;au=&amp;aulast=&amp;issn=10383433&amp;isbn=&amp;title=Herald+Sun&amp;jtitle=Herald+Sun&amp;pubname=Herald+Sun&amp;btitle=&amp;atitle=Herald+Sun&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=50&amp;date=2021&amp;doi=&amp;sid=ProQuest

Full text: Oh, for leaders with vision ALL Victorians, along with our fellow Australians, are entitled to feel confused and miserable.
We can’t know what next week holds, much less next month or next year. Some countries have plans for the uncertainty of Covid. Unlike Australia, such countries boast a unity of purpose and transparency of approach that seeks to protect the vulnerable while also avoiding the growing emotional and economic costs of lockdowns.
Take Israel, a world leader in the fight, where the people are receiving booster shots and lockdowns are now considered “a last resort”.
Prime Minister Naftali Bennett has outlined the actions of his government, and the responsibility of his citizens, in the pursuit of “safe shores” (see opposite page).
He has explained the challenges, and the necessary responses, with candour and singular intent. He offers evidence for his conclusions. Better yet, he gives his people hope.
“It would be easy to declare a lockdown and give money to closed businesses and the workers sitting at home,” he said. “But the policy of lockdowns has a terrible price … “Let’s think about the number of motor vehicle accidents. It would be possible to reduce to zero the number of fatalities from traffic accidents by banning travel on Israel’s highways. But we all understand that we have to live and allow traffic in Israel. Thus it is with the Delta strain. Daily routine is part of life and we must find a responsible balance.” Mr Bennett puts a price on lockdowns. The children who might miss out on lifesaving operations. The MRI machines that would go unfinanced. The pensioners who could not be paid. And the staggering debt that will seep through generations.
“We are the only country in which vaccines are available for everyone and the only one in which there is a third dose, a booster,” he said. “This is not a given and it is possible thanks for our hard work.” Contrast his thinking with Australia, where our two biggest cities face indefinite lockdowns, and a federal government has been exposed, again and again, for reacting to crises as they happen, as opposed to anticipating challenges with long-term thinking.
Mixed messages dominate. According to NSW Premier Gladys Berejiklian, parts of Sydney can re-emerge when vaccination rates hit 70 per cent and Sydneysiders produce vaccination certificates.
On the other side of the country, West Australian Premier Mark McGowan will not guarantee a future free of lockdowns, even if the magic target of 80 per cent vaccinations is reached.
Here in Victoria, questions mount. Premier Dan Andrews called Friday “a bad day”. When was the last good day?
What will happen when vaccination rates hit 70 per cent, projected to be in November?
When will kids be able to go to school? When will the vaccinated be allowed to rediscover the many lost pleasures once taken for granted? And when will the damned curfew end?
For a brief time, the nation seemed to co-operate after the release of the Doherty plan, which spoke of a “national transition” to a “national Covid response” away from lockdowns.
The modelling was based on 70 and 80 per cent vaccination rates. University of Melbourne professor of mathematical biology James McCaw, who contributed to the study, said the road map was based on managing virus spread in the community.
“Effective management of circulating virus without lockdowns requires high vaccine coverage and it requires there not to be a lot of cases in the community,” he said.
Herein lies an obvious problem. In Melbourne, as well as Sydney, lockdowns have not stemmed exponential rises in case numbers. Knee-jerk responses dominate the projected courses. In Victoria, we lack any horizon of optimism.
In an open letter published on Friday, News Corp Australasia’s executive chairman Michael Miller called on all leaders to “champion the vaccination rollout and participate in it every way we can” to get the country “back to full operations”.
“We have to urgently rediscover our Australian sense of purpose and unity and show the world what a fully vaccinated, fully functioning nation can achieve,” Mr Miller said.
“People who have already endured so much disruption and upheaval do not deserve to be kept in a state of isolation and uncertainty without a clear path forward.” He warned the country was “falling behind”.
“I’m asking state and national leaders to agree on, and be fully committed to, national targets and a national timetable for when we can achieve a vaccination level that will allow all Australians to plan for life in a new normal state of affairs,” he said.
Other countries have such charters. They show that a shared promise of something better can be pursued in the Covid chaos.
Australia is far bigger than self-interested fiefdoms. Its leaders have steered courses through world wars so the country prospered in the aftermaths. They held the nation together with more than empty invocations of trust. When will our leaders get their act together, not only for its fatigued and confused people, but also for the generations ahead who will pay for the acute lack of vision in this time of crisis?

VIC LAGGING IN JAB RACE

Publication info: Herald Sun ; Melbourne, Vic. [Melbourne, Vic]. 11 Aug 2021: 9.

https://login.ezproxy.lib.rmit.edu.au/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/vic-lagging-jab-race/docview/2559927032/se-2?accountid=13552

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://libkey.io/libraries/1057/openurl?genre=article&amp;au=&amp;aulast=&amp;issn=10383433&amp;isbn=&amp;title=VIC+LAGGING+IN+JAB+RACE&amp;jtitle=Herald+Sun&amp;pubname=Herald+Sun&amp;btitle=&amp;atitle=VIC+LAGGING+IN+JAB+RACE&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=9&amp;date=2021&amp;doi=&amp;sid=ProQuest

Full text: EXCLUSIVE VICTORIAN doctors are ­ad­min­istering less than half as many vaccine doses as their NSW counterparts every day, leaving our state lagging in the race to meet targets to reopen.
Based on current averages, 70 per cent of Victorian adults will be fully vaccinated against Covid-19 by November 21 – five days after the nation reaches that critical mark to limit lockdowns and ease ­restrictions on the vaccinated.
Victoria is on pace to hit the 80 per cent target by December 13 – three weeks after NSW and also slower than Tasmania, the ACT and the Northern Territory. Under the national cabinet’s plan, states can progress through the reopening phases once the vaccine targets have been met both nationally and in their own jurisdictions.
In NSW, the vaccination rate has drastically increased amid its worsening outbreak. Over the past week, NSW GPs delivered 398,940 doses compared to 172,405 doses by Victorian GPs.
The performance of state-run vaccination facilities across the two biggest states has been more comparable, with the jab rate in Victoria’s mass hubs now being lifted by the rush of people under 40 getting the ­Astra­Zeneca vaccine.
The Herald Sun can reveal that since Scott Morrison open­ed the door at the end of June for under-40s to access AstraZeneca, 31,510 young Victorians have received a first dose and 12,635 have had both.
Premier Daniel Andrews said on Tuesday that Victorians aged 18-39 were making an appointment every seven seconds for the jab.
A total of 2366 first doses of AstraZeneca were administered in that age group on Monday, which Health Minister Martin Foley said was a three-fold increase on the previous Monday. More than 13,000 young Victorians have also booked in to be vaccinated since the state government at the weekend ­allow­ed hubs to start giving Astra­Zeneca to under 40s.
But Wyndham Council on Tuesday night urgently called on the state government to provide a local seven-day-a-week vaccination hub for 18-39 year-olds. Mayor Adele Hegedich said the LGA’s young people had been ignored.
“Fifty-six per cent of Wyndham’s 280,000 residents are under 35 and, as the recent outbreak has demonstrated, they desperately need access to vaccinations,” she said.
“Currently, the closest state-run vaccination hub is in Sunshine or Geelong.” In more concerning developments last night, the Caroline Springs Square Shopping Centre was deemed a Tier 1 exposure site from August 2 to August 5 for all staff.
Australian Medical Association state president Roderick McRae warned some GPs were feeling “jaded and fatigued”, and more had doses that were about to expire than problems obtaining more supplies. “Many GPs, and probably more their staff, have been abused by frustrated and angry people,” he said.
“They have had plenty of people cancel, particularly second AstraZeneca (doses).” Dr McRae said he expected the rollout would accelerate as ­eligibility was expanded. “One of the key mechanisms of us all getting out of these recurrent lockdowns is to have a high per­cent­age of [us] vaccinated.” University of South Australia biostatistics expert Professor Adrian Esterman said increasing supplies of Pfizer, coupled with the arrival of the Moderna vaccine next month, meant all jurisdictions would be able to significantly ramp up rollouts.
“The key there is not to rely on GPs but rely on pharmacists and vaccine hubs, which I’m sure the Victorian government will do,” he said. Victorians in their 30s are expected to get access to Pfizer and Moderna within weeks, in line with modelling from the Doherty Institute that showed younger adults were the most likely to spread the virus. Mr Foley said it was clear there was a “strong emphasis” among young people to get vaccinated.
“Clearly, young people have assessed their personal situation in a strong and informed manner and many are determining that there are significant risks in remaining unvac­cinated during the Delta out­break, and during lockdown, and they’ve made a ­decision where their interests and the community’s interest lies,” he said.
“They’re doing it for themselves, for their family and for their local community and cultural organisations ”.
tom.minear@news.com.au MARK KNIGHT, PAGE 21

NT News TIME TO END VACCINE UNCERTAINTY

Publication info: The Northern Territory News ; Darwin, N.T. [Darwin, N.T]. 11 Aug 2021: 34.

https://login.ezproxy.lib.rmit.edu.au/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/nt-news-time-end-vaccine-uncertainty/docview/2559926889/se-2?accountid=13552

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://libkey.io/libraries/1057/openurl?genre=article&amp;au=&amp;aulast=&amp;issn=18373909&amp;isbn=&amp;title=NT+News+TIME+TO+END+VACCINE+UNCERTAINTY&amp;jtitle=The+Northern+Territory+News&amp;pubname=The+Northern+Territory+News&amp;btitle=&amp;atitle=NT+News+TIME+TO+END+VACCINE+UNCERTAINTY&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=34&amp;date=2021&amp;doi=&amp;sid=ProQuest

Full text: CHIEF Minister Michael Gunner’s decision to break ranks with his fellow state and territory leaders and go out on his own in terms of when to end lockdowns in the Territory is clearly driven by concern for our most vulnerable.
“If remote communities require a higher rate of vaccination, that impacts my policy decisions going forward,” he said.
“I need really high vaccination rates here, probably higher than the Doherty Institute is flagging in their modelling.” It’s hard to argue with that assessment, the Territory’s many remote Indigenous communities are uniquely vulnerable to the ravages of Covid-19 and if the insidious disease were to find a foothold in any of them it could be disastrous on a grand scale.
So it makes sense that the 70 per cent vaccination target set for the rest of the nation might not be appropriate for the NT.
But all that said, it’s not good enough to have no plan at all. While most Territorians will readily accept the need to protect the most vulnerable in our community is rightly Mr Gunner’s foremost consideration, some vision for the future is also required.
If 70 per cent is too low then fine. If the figure is more than 80 per cent then so be it. But whatever it is, we need to work it out sooner rather than later so we can all get back to normal.

Subject: Immunization

Title: NT News TIME TO END VACCINE UNCERTAINTY

Publication title: The Northern Territory News; Darwin, N.T.

First page: 34

VIC LAGGING IN JAB RACE

Publication info: Herald Sun ; Melbourne, Vic. [Melbourne, Vic]. 11 Aug 2021: 9.

https://login.ezproxy.lib.rmit.edu.au/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/vic-lagging-jab-race/docview/2559926243/se-2?accountid=13552

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://libkey.io/libraries/1057/openurl?genre=article&amp;au=&amp;aulast=&amp;issn=10383433&amp;isbn=&amp;title=VIC+LAGGING+IN+JAB+RACE&amp;jtitle=Herald+Sun&amp;pubname=Herald+Sun&amp;btitle=&amp;atitle=VIC+LAGGING+IN+JAB+RACE&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=9&amp;date=2021&amp;doi=&amp;sid=ProQuest

Full text: EXCLUSIVE VICTORIAN doctors are ­administering less than half as many vaccine doses as their New South Wales counterparts every day, leaving our state lagging behind in the race to meet targets to reopen.
Based on current averages, 70 per cent of Victorian adults will be fully vaccinated against Covid-19 by November 21 — five days after the nation reaches that critical mark to limit lockdowns and ease ­restrictions on the vaccinated.
Victoria is on pace to hit the 80 per cent target by December 13, three weeks after New South Wales and also slower than Tasmania, the ACT and the Northern Territory.
Under the national cabinet’s plan, states can progress through the reopening phases once the vaccine targets have been met both nationally and in their own jurisdictions.
In NSW, the vaccination rate has drastically increased amid its worsening outbreak. Over the past week, New South Wales GPs delivered 398,940 doses, compared to 172,405 doses by Victorian GPs.
The performance of state-run vaccination facilities across the two biggest states has been more comparable, with the jab rate in Victoria’s mass hubs now being lifted by the rush of people under 40 coming forward to get the ­AstraZeneca vaccine.
The Herald Sun can reveal that since Scott Morrison opened the door for under-40s to access AstraZeneca at the end of June, 31,510 young Victorians have received a first dose and 12,635 have had both.
Premier Daniel Andrews said on Tuesday Victorians aged 18-39 were making an appointment every seven seconds for the jab.
A total of 2366 first doses of AstraZeneca were administered to people in that age group on Monday, which Health Minister Martin Foley said was a three-fold increase on the previous Monday.
More than 13,000 young Victorians have also booked in to be vaccinated since the state government at the weekend ­allowed it hubs to start giving AstraZeneca to under 40s.
Australian Medical Association state president Roderick McRae warned some GPs were feeling “jaded and fatigued”, and more had doses that were about to expire than problems obtaining more supplies.
“Many GPs and probably more their staff have been abused by frustrated and angry people,” Dr McRae said.
“They have had plenty of people cancel, particularly second AstraZeneca (doses).” He said he expected the rollout would accelerate as ­eligibility was expanded to more Victorians.
“Drive-throughs appear to be successful and they cause enthusiasm,” Dr McRae said.
“One of the key mechanisms of us all getting out of these recurrent lockdowns is to have a high percentage of the community vaccinated.” University of South Australia biostatistics expert Professor Adrian Esterman said increasing supplies of Pfizer, coupled with the arrival of the Moderna vaccine next month, meant all jurisdictions would be able to significantly ramp up their vaccine rollouts.
“The key there is not to rely on GPs but rely on pharmacists and vaccine hubs, which I’m sure the Victorian government will do,” he said, adding pharmacists should have been part of the rollout sooner.
Victorians in their 30s are expected to get access to Pfizer and Moderna within weeks, in line with modelling from the Doherty Institute which showed younger adults were the most likely to spread the virus. Mr Foley said it was clear there was a “strong emphasis” among young people to get vaccinated.
“Clearly, young people have assessed their personal situation in a strong and informed manner and many are determining that there are significant risks in remaining unvaccinated during the Delta outbreak, and during lockdown, and they’ve made a ­decision as to where their interests and the community’s interest lies,” he said.
“They’re doing it for themselves, doing it for their family and doing it for their local community and sporting clubs and cultural organisations.” tom.minear@news.com.au MARK KNIGHT, PAGE 21

Cabinet refuses to back bosses ordering workers to get jab

Publication info: The Daily Telegraph ; Surry Hills, N.S.W. [Surry Hills, N.S.W]. 07 Aug 2021: 9.

https://login.ezproxy.lib.rmit.edu.au/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/cabinet-refuses-back-bosses-ordering-workers-get/docview/2558840809/se-2?accountid=13552

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://libkey.io/libraries/1057/openurl?genre=article&amp;au=&amp;aulast=&amp;issn=18360203&amp;isbn=&amp;title=Cabinet+refuses+to+back+bosses+ordering+workers+to+get+jab&amp;jtitle=The+Daily+Telegraph&amp;pubname=The+Daily+Telegraph&amp;btitle=&amp;atitle=Cabinet+refuses+to+back+bosses+ordering+workers+to+get+jab&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=9&amp;date=2021&amp;doi=&amp;sid=ProQuest

Full text: WE can’t end lockdowns without vaccination but National Cabinet has decided not to set rules about who can be ordered to get the jab.
Now Scott Morrison has warned bosses wanting to force their workers to get the jab must comply with federal discrimination legislation.
National Cabinet on Friday considered advice from the federal Solicitor-General Stephen Donaghue cautioning that under current federal legislation employers will need to tread carefully about how they ­instruct employees about vaccination or they could easily find themselves in court.
And it stepped back from making any changes to the rules that would make it clear to Australians in what circumstances employers can order their staff to take the jab.
This is despite major firms in the US and UK ordering staff to get the jab. The PM said the question of what bosses were allowed to do would turn on the question of reasonableness.
He said that “ultimately employers need to consider these matters and make their own decisions, remembering we do not have a mandatory vaccination policy in this country. We do not have that. We’re not proposing to have that. That is not changing. An employer may make a reasonable directive to staff” but any employer making such a ­direction “will have to stay consistent with the law”.
So far the only workers required to get vaccinated for their jobs have been done so under states’ public health orders.
In other developments Nat­ional Cabinet has now “fully agreed” to live with the virus once 70 per cent of the population is vaccinated. Under the Doherty Institute’s modelling, lockdowns should be abandoned when the country reaches 80 per cent vaccination.
But despite “extensive discussions”, Cabinet stepped back from allowing more extensive use of rapid antigen testing, which would allow employers to test staff each day for Covid. The PM said their use would be more appropriate after the current suppression phase.
CREDIT: James Campbell

Pollies getting snappy

Publication info: Herald Sun ; Melbourne, Vic. [Melbourne, Vic]. 06 Aug 2021: 8.

https://login.ezproxy.lib.rmit.edu.au/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/pollies-getting-snappy/docview/2558255138/se-2?accountid=13552

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://libkey.io/libraries/1057/openurl?genre=article&amp;au=&amp;aulast=&amp;issn=10383433&amp;isbn=&amp;title=Pollies+getting+snappy&amp;jtitle=Herald+Sun&amp;pubname=Herald+Sun&amp;btitle=&amp;atitle=Pollies+getting+snappy&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=8&amp;date=2021&amp;doi=&amp;sid=ProQuest

Full text: ANALYSIS THE Delta variant moves rapidly – and so does the political response. It was barely a month ago that Premier Daniel Andrews, in his first week back at work after his injury, signed up to the national cabinet’s plan to “transition” the nation’s approach to Covid-19.
In black and white, the leaders declared lockdowns were “to be used only as a last resort”.
Victoria is now locked down for the second time since then. We will wake on Friday to kids missing school, shop owners shutting their doors, florists throwing out flowers, lovers cancelling weddings.
And instead of criticism from Canberra, the Premier’s hard-and-fast approach is fully backed by Scott Morrison — because it’s now the national cabinet’s preferred response. Last Friday, they changed their minds and agreed that until at least 70 per cent of the adult population was fully vaccinated, state leaders should pull the trigger on “early, stringent and short lockdowns if outbreaks occur”.
Announcing Victoria’s sixth lockdown on Thursday evening — barely 32 hours after celebrating a zero-case day — Andrews said with a handful of mystery cases, he simply had “no alternative”.
“We only get one chance to go fast,” he said, warning that the state could otherwise be locked down until Christmas without the snap shutdown.
Front of mind for Andrews, the Prime Minister and their national cabinet colleagues is the Sydney experience, where Gladys Berejiklian held out on harsh restrictions and inadvertently fuelled a public health bushfire that seems likely to run for weeks – if not months – to come. In the face of the Delta variant, snap lockdowns are now backed by the Doherty Institute as the right health response and the federal Treasury as the right economic response – endorsements Andrews was quick to cite on Thursday.
That will be cold comfort for Victorians, who have now spent more than six months in lockdown in the 500 days since Australia was first frozen to “flatten the curve”.
In the darkest days of last year’s deadly second wave, Andrews maintained the state would gain from that pain.
“We won’t spend 2021 bouncing in and out of lockdowns,” he promised. And yet here we are.
The Doherty Institute’s modelling makes it clear that we have little chance of controlling the Delta variant and preventing widespread loss of life until far more Australians are vaccinated.
That means our sixth lockdown is unlikely to be our last. It must also mean that the federal government throws absolutely everything into fast-tracking the delivery of every single dose it can get its hands on.
Victorians are a remarkably resilient bunch but there is only so much they can take. They have done everything governments have asked them to do – it’s well past time their sacrifices were repaid with vaccines for all. tom.minear@news.com.au
CREDIT: TOM MINEAR
